A Ruler's Oath to Inform About Troublemakers
Explains that a ruler's oath to report troublemakers is tied to his tenure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a ruler's oath to inform about troublemakers is tied to his authority and apply the same principle to analogous cases involving creditors, husbands, and sultans.

Core Idea
An oath restrained by authority: when the ruler's power ends, the oath ends—because its purpose is to enable action, not bind beyond reach.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author explores a critical Hanafi principle: oaths are interpreted according to their purpose, not just their literal wording. When a ruler swears a subordinate to report troublemakers, the oath is automatically tied to the ruler's authority. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its proof by istihsan, and its application to analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
State the rule: the oath administered by a ruler to inform about troublemakers is restricted to the duration of the ruler's authority (taqayyadat biqiyam wilayatihi). It becomes void upon death or dismissal.
Note the distinction between wording (absolute) and meaning (restricted by purpose).
2
Understand the basis: purpose vs. wording
Explain why the Hanafi school uses istihsan to restrict the absolute wording: the purpose of the oath—to enable the ruler to deter evil—cannot be achieved after authority ends. Thus, the oath is implicitly tied to tenure.
The proof is dalil aqli (rational) based on custom and purpose, not explicit text.
3
Analyze the analogies (qiyas)
The text extends the same reasoning to three analogous cases: (1) creditor's oath for debtor, (2) husband's oath for wife, (3) sultan's oath for subject. In each, the existence of a relationship (debt, marriage, sultanate) that confers the right to prevent is the illa for restricting the oath.
Note: In the husband case, if the oath is not explicitly tied to marriage (e.g., a conditional divorce upon exit), it remains binding after divorce because the illa of permission is absent.
4
Consider the promotion scenario
The author notes an unresolved issue: if the official is promoted to a higher authority, the oath likely remains valid because his ability to remove corruption has increased, thus the purpose is still achievable.
This demonstrates the principle's flexibility: the oath's validity depends on whether the purpose can still be fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو حلفه وال ليعلمنه بكل داعر دخل البلد تقيد بقيام ولايته بيان لكون اليمين المطلقة تصير مقيدة من جهة المعنى كما في هذه المسألة لأنها مطلقة من حيث اللفظ لكن لما كان مقصود المستحلف دفع شره أو شر غيره بزجره فلا يفيد فائدته بعد زوال سلطنته والزوال بالموت، وكذا بالعزل في ظاهر الرواية. والداعر بالدال والعين المهملتين كل مفسد وجمعه دعار من الدعر وهو الفساد، ومنه دعر العود يدعر بكسر العين في الماضي وفتحها في المضارع إذا فسد. تقيدت بقيام ولايته بطلت اليمين بعزله فلا تعود بعد توليته. ولم يذكر المصنف أن اليمين على الفور أو التراخي. وفي التبيين : ثم إن الحالف لو علم الداعر ولم يعلمه لم يحنث إلا إذا مات أو المستحلف أو عزل لأنه لا يحنث في اليمين المطلقة بمجرد الترك بل باليأس عن الفعل، وذلك بما ذكرنا إلا إذا كانت مؤقتة فيحنث بمضي الوقت مع الإمكان وإلا فلا اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : ولو حكم بانعقاد هذه للفور لم يكن بعيداً نظراً إلى المقصود وهي المبادرة لزجره ودفع شره، فالدعر يوجب التقييد بالفور وفور علمه به .اهـ وليس العموم في قوله بكل داعر» على بابه لأنه لا يمكنه أن يعلمه بكل داعر في الدنيا، وإنما مراده كل داعر يعرفه أو في بلده أو دخل البلد، وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى مسائل منها لو حلف رب الدين غريمه أو الكفيل بأمر المكفول عنه أن لا يخرج من البلد إلا بإذنه تقيد بالخروج حال قيام الدين والكفالة لأن الإذن إنما يصح ممن له ولاية المنع وولاية المنع حال قيامه ومنها لو حلف لا تخرج امرأته إلا بإذنه تقيد بحال قيام الزوجية بخلاف ما إذا قال إن خرجت امرأته . من هذه الدار فعبده حر ولم يقيده بالإذن أو حلف لا يقبلها فخرجت بعد ما أبانها أو قبلها بعدما أبانها حيث يحنث لأنه لم يوجد فيه دلالة التقييد في حال قيام الزوجية. وعلى هذا لو قال لامرأته كل امرأة أتزوجها بغير إذنك طالق فطلق امرأته طلاقاً بائناً أو ثلاثاً ثم تزوج بغير إذنها طلقت لأنه لم يقيد يمينه ببقاء النكاح لأنها إنما تتقيد به لو كانت المرأة تستفيد ولاية الإذن والمنع بعقد النكاح. ومنها لو أن سلطاناً حلف رجلاً أن لا يخرج من البلد إلا بإذنه ثم خرج بعد عزله بدون إذنه لا يحنث لأن اليمين تقيدت بحال قيام السلطنة؛ كذا في المحيط. ولم أر حكم ما إذا حلفه وال ليعلمنه بكل داعر ثم عزل من وظيفته وتولى وظيفة أخرى أعلى منها كالدويدار إذا حلف حقيراً ثم صار والياً وهو المسمى في زماننا بالصوباشاه، وينبغي أن لا يبطل اليمين لأنه صار متمكناً من إزالة الفساد أكثر من الحالة الأولى
His statement: (And if a governor made him swear to inform him of every troublemaker entering the city, it is tied to the continuation of his authority) as an explanation that an unrestricted oath becomes restricted from the perspective of meaning, as in this issue. Because it is unrestricted in wording, but since the purpose of the one administering the oath is to repel his evil or the evil of others by deterring him, it does not serve its benefit after his authority ends, and ending is by death, and similarly by dismissal in the apparent narration. The word 'da'ir' with unpointed daal and 'ayn means every corrupt person, its plural is du'aaar, from da'ar meaning corruption, and from it da'ara al-'ud yad'aru (with kasra of the 'ayn in past and fatha in present) when it becomes corrupt. It is tied to the continuation of his authority; the oath becomes void by his dismissal and does not return after his reappointment. The author did not mention whether the oath is immediate or delayed. And in al-Tabyin: Then if the swearer knew of the troublemaker and did not inform him, he does not break his oath unless the swearer dies, the one who administered the oath dies, or he is dismissed, because in an unrestricted oath he does not break merely by leaving but by despair of the action, and that is as we mentioned, unless it is time-bound, then he breaks by the passing of time with possibility, otherwise not. And in Fath al-Qadir: And if one ruled that this oath is immediate, it would not be far-fetched, considering the purpose which is to hasten to deter him and repel his evil, because corruption necessitates restriction to immediacy and immediacy of his knowledge of it. And the generality in his statement 'every troublemaker' is not taken literally because he cannot inform him of every troublemaker in the world; rather he means every troublemaker he knows or in his city or who enters the city. And the author indicated issues: among them, if the creditor made his debtor or the guarantor by order of the guaranteed swear not to leave the city without his permission, it is tied to leaving while the debt and guarantee exist, because permission is only valid from one who has authority to prevent and his authority exists during its continuation. Among them, if he swore that his wife would not go out except with his permission, it is tied to the state of marriage, unlike if he said 'If my wife goes out of this house, my slave is free' and did not tie it to permission, or he swore not to kiss her and she went out after he divorced her irrevocably, or kissed her after such divorce, where he breaks his oath because there is no indication of restriction to the state of marriage. And accordingly, if he said to his wife 'Every woman I marry without your permission is divorced', then he divorced his wife irrevocably or three times, then married without her permission, she is divorced because he did not restrict his oath to the continuation of marriage, since it would only be restricted if the woman gains the authority of permission and prevention by the marriage contract. Among them, if a sultan made a man swear not to leave the city without his permission, then he left after his dismissal without permission, he does not break his oath because the oath is tied to the state of sultanate; as in al-Muhit. And I have not seen the ruling for if a governor made him swear to inform him of every troublemaker, then he was dismissed from his post and assumed another higher post, like a doorkeeper who swore when he was lowly then became a governor, which is called in our time 'al-subashah'; it should be that the oath is not voided because he became more able to remove corruption than the first state.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of an oath administered by a ruler to inform him of all troublemakers. Although the oath's wording is absolute (muthlaq), its meaning is restricted by the ruler's authority (wilaya). The oath becomes valid only during the ruler's tenure; if he dies or is dismissed, the oath becomes void and does not revive if he is reinstated. The text explains that the purpose of the oath is to enable the ruler to deter evil, which only makes sense while he holds power. It also clarifies that the term 'every troublemaker' is not literal—it applies only to those known to the swearer or within the city. The passage then draws analogies: a creditor's oath regarding a debtor is tied to the debt's existence; a husband's oath about his wife's exit is tied to marriage; a sultan's oath about a subject's departure is tied to his sultanate. A notable point: if the official is promoted to a higher position, the oath likely remains valid because his ability to prevent evil has increased. This shows the Hanafi principle that oaths are construed according to custom and purpose (al-yamin tu'awwilu 'ala al-'urf wa al-qasd).

Key Terms
داعر (da'ir): A corrupt person or troublemaker; from d'a'r meaning corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A governor swears a subordinate to inform him of every troublemaker entering the city. The governor is then dismissed. What is the ruling on the oath?
A) The oath remains binding because it was absolute.
B) The oath becomes void because it was tied to the governor's authority.
C) The oath continues only if the subordinate had already started informing.
D) The oath becomes binding on the new governor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets oaths by their purpose: a ruler's oath to inform is valid only as long as he has the authority to act on that information.

Oath to Give a Gift: Fulfilled Without Acceptance
Ruling on oath fulfillment for gift vs. sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths to give gifts and sales, and apply the condition of acceptance to determine oath fulfillment.

Core Idea
A gift oath is fulfilled by offer alone; a sale oath needs acceptance.

In Hanafi fiqh, the completion of a contract affects the fulfillment of oaths. This passage clarifies that a gift (hiba) is completed by the mere offer (ijab), while a sale (bay') requires acceptance (qabul). Consequently, someone who swears to give a gift fulfills the oath by offering it, even if the recipient does not accept. This lesson explains the ruling, the rationale, and the practical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the text: if someone swears to give a gift and offers it without acceptance, the oath is fulfilled. If they swear to sell and the buyer does not accept, the oath is not fulfilled.
The key comparison is between hiba and bay'.
2
Understand the Hanafi definition of gift
According to the Hanafi school, a gift is completed by the offer (ijab) alone. Acceptance (qabul) is not an integral part (rukn) of the contract. This is why the oath is fulfilled upon offering.
Other schools require acceptance and possession.
3
Contrast with sale
A sale (bay') is not completed until the buyer accepts the offer. Therefore, an oath to sell is not fulfilled until the offer is accepted.
The sale contract requires both offer and acceptance.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine whether the oath is fulfilled by checking if the act (gift or sale) is legally complete according to Hanafi rules.
For gift: only offer needed; for sale: offer and acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (يبر بالهبة بلا قبول بخلاف البيع) فإذا حلف ليهبن فلاناً فوهب له فلم يقبل فإنه يبر، ولو حلف ليبيعن كذا فباعه فلم يقبل لم يبر، لأن الهبة تتم بالإيجاب عندنا، والبيع لا يتم إلا بالقبول.
His statement: (He is released by a gift without acceptance, unlike sale). So if he swore to give something as a gift to someone, and he gave it but the recipient did not accept, he is released. And if he swore to sell something, and he sold but the other did not accept, he is not released, because a gift is completed by offer according to us, whereas a sale is not completed except by acceptance.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage states a clear Hanafi ruling regarding oaths to perform a contract. If someone swears to make a gift (hiba) to another, the oath is fulfilled by the act of offering (ijab) alone, even if the donee does not accept. This is because in the Hanafi school, a gift is completed by the offer without need for acceptance (qabul), as ownership transfers upon offer. In contrast, a sale (bay') is not completed without acceptance by the buyer. Therefore, an oath to sell is not fulfilled until the buyer accepts, even if the seller makes an offer. This distinction is based on the nature of each contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift; in Hanafi law, completed by offer without acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone swears 'By Allah, I will give my book to Ahmad.' He offers the book to Ahmad, but Ahmad does not accept. According to the Hanafi madhhab, has he fulfilled his oath?
A) Yes, because a gift is completed by offer alone.
B) No, because acceptance is required.
C) Yes, only if Ahmad is present.
D) No, because the book was not handed over.

Key Takeaway
A gift oath is fulfilled by the mere offer; a sale oath requires acceptance to be fulfilled.

Oath Concerning a Dā'ir (Mischief-maker)
Analyzing the conditions for an oath against a dā'ir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an oath concerning a dā'ir becomes void and explain the meaning of 'every dā'ir' in the context of the oath.

Core Idea
An oath to inform about a dā'ir is conditioned on the administrator's authority; upon death or dismissal, the oath becomes void.

In this passage, the commentator explores a specific oath administered to a person to report any mischief-maker (dā'ir). The ruling hinges on the purpose of the oath: to deter harm. When the administrator's authority ends—by death or removal—the oath can no longer fulfill its purpose, so it becomes void. This lesson unpacks the rationale, the lexical definition of dā'ir, and the scope of the term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? It is an oath administered to a person to inform about any dā'ir. The ruling is that the oath is binding (wajib) and conditioned on the administrator's authority.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The purpose of the oath is to repel harm (دفع الشر). This purpose cannot be fulfilled once the authority ceases.
The dalil is a rational consideration, not a specific text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The meaning shows that the oath's effectiveness depends on the administrator's ability to act. When he dies or is dismissed, the oath becomes futile, so it becomes void.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a difference of opinion on whether the oath is for immediate action or allows delay. At-Tabyīn holds it is not immediate (he only breaks upon despair), while Fatḥ al-Qadīr suggests it should be immediate given the objective.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position is that the oath becomes void upon death or dismissal of the administrator. The generality of 'every dā'ir' is not literal; it refers to those known or in the city.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا جهة المعنى كما في هذه المسألة لأنها مطلقة من حيث اللفظ لكن لما كان مقصود المستحلف دفع شره أو شر غيره بزجره فلا يفيد فائدته بعد زوال سلطنته والزوال بالموت، وكذا بالعزل في ظاهر الرواية. والداعر بالدال والعين المهملتين كل مفسد وجمعه دعار من الدعر وهو الفساد، ومنه دعر العود يدعر بكسر العين في الماضي وفتحها في المضارع إذا فسد. تقيدت بقيام ولايته بطلت اليمين بعزله فلا تعود بعد توليته. ولم يذكر المصنف أن اليمين على الفور أو التراخي. وفي التبيين : ثم إن الحالف لو علم الداعر ولم يعلمه لم يحنث إلا إذا مات أو المستحلف أو عزل لأنه لا يحنث في اليمين المطلقة بمجرد الترك بل باليأس عن الفعل، وذلك بما ذكرنا إلا إذا كانت مؤقتة فيحنث بمضي الوقت مع الإمكان وإلا فلا اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : ولو حكم بانعقاد هذه للفور لم يكن بعيداً نظراً إلى المقصود وهي المبادرة لزجره ودفع شره، فالدعر يوجب التقييد بالفور وفور علمه به .اهـ وليس العموم في قوله بكل داعر» على بابه لأنه لا يمكنه أن يعلمه بكل داعر في الدنيا، وإنما مراده كل داعر يعرفه أو في بلده أو دخل البلد
And it is based on meaning as in this issue, because it is unrestricted in wording, but since the purpose of the one administering the oath is to repel his evil or the evil of others by deterring him, it does not achieve its benefit after his authority ceases, and cessation occurs by death, and likewise by dismissal according to the apparent narration. 'Ad-dā'ir' with the letter dal and 'ayn unpointed is every corrupt person, its plural is du'ār, from ad-da'ar which is corruption, and from it da'ara al-‘ūd yad'aru with kasra of the ‘ayn in the past and fatha in the present when it becomes corrupt. It is restricted to the existence of his authority; the oath is invalidated by his dismissal and does not return after his appointment. And the author did not mention that the oath is immediate or delayed. And in at-Tabyīn: Then if the one who swore knew the dā'ir and did not inform, he does not break the oath unless the dā'ir dies, or the one who administered the oath dies or is dismissed, because in an unrestricted oath he does not break it by mere abandonment but by despair of the action, and that is by what we mentioned unless it was time-bound, then he breaks it when the time passes with possibility, otherwise not. And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: If it were ruled that this is immediate, it would not be far-fetched considering the objective, which is to hasten to deter him and repel his evil, so the corruption necessitates restriction to immediacy upon his knowledge of it. And the generality in his statement 'every dā'ir' is not taken literally because he cannot inform on every dā'ir in the world, rather his intent is every dā'ir he knows, or in his city, or who enters the city.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing an oath administered to a person to inform about any dā'ir (mischief-maker). The commentator explains that the oath is tied to the authority of the person who administered it. If the authority dies or is removed from office, the oath becomes void because its purpose—to deter the mischief-maker—can no longer be achieved. The text defines 'dā'ir' lexically as any corrupt person. The commentator notes that the author (of the matn) did not specify whether the oath is for immediate action or allows delay. He then cites at-Tabyīn, which explains that in an unrestricted oath, breaking occurs only when there is no possibility of action, such as when the person dies or the authority is removed. However, if the oath is time-bound, breaking occurs when the time expires without action. The commentator adds from Fatḥ al-Qadīr that considering the objective, the oath should be for immediate action. Finally, he clarifies that the word 'every' in 'every dā'ir' is not general; it refers only to those the person knows about or those in his city, as it is impossible to inform about all mischief-makers worldwide.

Key Terms
الداعر (ad-dā'ir): A corrupt person who causes mischief.
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamīn al-muṭlaqah): An oath that is not restricted by a time condition or other qualifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The person breaks the oath and must perform kaffārah.
B) The oath becomes void and no kaffārah is due.
C) The person must inform the family of the administrator.
D) The oath continues and must be fulfilled within one year.

Key Takeaway
An oath to report a dā'ir is void when the administrator's authority ends, and 'every dā'ir' is interpreted contextually, not literally.

Oaths Conditioned on Permission: The Principle of Wilayat al-Man‘
Explaining that oaths conditioned on permission are tied to the existence of the right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that oaths conditioned on permission are tied to the existence of the right giving authority to prevent, and apply it to scenarios involving debts, marriage, and public office.

Core Idea
An oath requiring permission is valid only as long as the permission-giver holds the authority to prevent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq explains a key Hanafi rule for interpreting oaths. When someone swears not to do something without permission, the oath is inherently linked to the period during which the permission-giver has the right to prevent the act. This principle yields specific rulings for debts, marriage, and even state authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and permission condition
Examine the wording of the oath to see if it is linked to permission from a specific person.
e.g., 'You will not leave the city except with my permission.'
2
Identify the underlying right giving authority to prevent
Determine the legal basis (debt, marriage, public office) that grants the permission-giver the power to prevent the action.
The authority is called wilāyat al-man‘.
3
Determine if the right currently exists
Check whether the debt, marriage, or official position is still in effect at the time of the potential action.
If the right has ended, the authority to prevent is lost.
4
Rule on the oath's applicability
If the right exists, the oath binds; if it has ended, the oath does not apply (no sin or expiation).
This applies unless the oath is explicitly not tied to the right's continuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى مسائل منها لو حلف رب الدين غريمه أو الكفيل بأمر المكفول عنه أن لا يخرج من البلد إلا بإذنه تقيد بالخروج حال قيام الدين والكفالة لأن الإذن إنما يصح ممن له ولاية المنع وولاية المنع حال قيامه ومنها لو حلف لا تخرج امرأته إلا بإذنه تقيد بحال قيام الزوجية بخلاف ما إذا قال إن خرجت امرأته . من هذه الدار فعبده حر ولم يقيده بالإذن أو حلف لا يقبلها فخرجت بعد ما أبانها أو قبلها بعدما أبانها حيث يحنث لأنه لم يوجد فيه دلالة التقييد في حال قيام الزوجية. وعلى هذا لو قال لامرأته كل امرأة أتزوجها بغير إذنك طالق فطلق امرأته طلاقاً بائناً أو ثلاثاً ثم تزوج بغير إذنها طلقت لأنه لم يقيد يمينه ببقاء النكاح لأنها إنما تتقيد به لو كانت المرأة تستفيد ولاية الإذن والمنع بعقد النكاح. ومنها لو أن سلطاناً حلف رجلاً أن لا يخرج من البلد إلا بإذنه ثم خرج بعد عزله بدون إذنه لا يحنث لأن اليمين تقيدت بحال قيام السلطنة؛ كذا في المحيط. ولم أر حكم ما إذا حلفه وال ليعلمنه بكل داعر ثم عزل من وظيفته وتولى وظيفة أخرى أعلى منها كالدويدار إذا حلف حقيراً ثم صار والياً وهو المسمى في زماننا بالصوباشاه، وينبغي أن لا يبطل اليمين لأنه صار متمكناً من إزالة الفساد أكثر من الحالة الأولى
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) pointed to issues, among them: If the creditor makes his debtor swear, or the guarantor makes the one for whom he guaranteed swear, not to leave the city except with his permission, it is restricted to leaving while the debt and guarantee exist, because permission is only valid from someone who has the authority to prevent, and the authority to prevent exists while it [the right] exists. Among them: If a man swears that his wife will not leave except with his permission, it is restricted to the existence of the marriage, unlike if he said, 'If my wife leaves this house, my slave is free' and did not restrict it with permission, or if he swore not to kiss her and she left after he divorced her irrevocably, or he kissed her after irrevocable divorce, where he breaks the oath, because there is no indication of restriction to the existence of marriage. Based on this, if he said to his wife, 'Every woman I marry without your permission is divorced,' and he divorced his wife with a final or triple divorce, then he married without her permission, she is divorced because he did not restrict his oath to the continuance of marriage; she would only be restricted by it if the woman acquires the authority of permission and prevention through the marriage contract. Among them: If a sultan makes a man swear not to leave the city except with his permission, then he leaves after his dismissal without his permission, he does not break the oath because the oath is restricted to the existence of sultanate; thus in al-Muhīt. And I have not seen the ruling if a wali made him swear to inform him of every dā'ir, then he was dismissed from his post and assumed another higher post, like a low-ranking official who becomes a wali, which in our time is called al-Ṣūbāshāh, and it is fitting that the oath is not invalidated because he becomes more capable of removing corruption than the first situation.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage presents a set of rulings regarding oaths that are conditioned on permission. The key principle is that such oaths are tied to the existence of the right that gives the person granting permission the authority to prevent the action. For example, if a creditor makes his debtor swear not to leave the city without permission, the oath applies only while the debt remains. Similarly, if a husband swears that his wife will not leave without permission, it is valid only as long as the marriage exists. However, if the oath is not explicitly tied to permission (e.g., 'If my wife leaves, my slave is free'), the oath remains in effect even after divorce because the condition is not linked to the marriage. The commentator also discusses a case involving a sultan: if a sultan swears a person not to leave without permission, and the sultan is later dismissed, the oath becomes void because it was conditioned on his authority. Finally, the commentator raises a new issue about a wali who is promoted; he opines that the oath should not be invalidated because the new authority is greater.

Key Terms
ولاية المنع (wilāyat al-man‘): The authority to prevent someone from doing something, here tied to a right like debt or marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a husband who swears 'Every woman I marry without your permission is divorced' cause divorce when he remarries after a final divorce?
A) Because the wife's authority to give permission continues after divorce.
B) Because the oath is not restricted to the continuance of marriage.
C) Because remarriage after divorce is invalid.
D) Because the husband intended to divorce the new wife.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets oaths conditioned on permission as tied to the legal basis of the permission-giver's authority; when that basis ends, the oath ceases to apply.

Definition of Rayḥān in Oaths
Clarifying the juristic definition of rayḥān in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath not to smell rayḥān and explain why smelling rose or jasmine does not break the oath, based on the juristic definition of rayḥān.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'rayḥān' is defined by juristic convention, not general language, so roses and jasmine are excluded.

This passage clarifies a nuance in oath rulings: if someone swears not to smell rayḥān (sweet basil), does smelling a rose or jasmine violate the oath? The Hanafi jurists explain that the term 'rayḥān' has a specific meaning in their legal discourse, which excludes roses and jasmine. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on oaths and avoiding unnecessary expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The author states that smelling rose or jasmine does not break the oath. What is the explicit ruling?
The ruling is a direct statement from the base text.
2
Analyze the juristic definition of rayḥān
According to Hanafi jurists, rayḥān is defined as a plant with a stem and pleasant scent. Identify the alternative definitions mentioned (Iraqi custom: no stem; or no tree).
These definitions aim to limit the term to certain plants.
3
Determine inclusion/exclusion
Based on these definitions, are roses and jasmine included? Why not?
Roses and jasmine lack the characteristic stem or specific structure.
4
Consider conventional understanding in practice
The commentator cites Fatḥ al-Qadīr, emphasizing reliance on local convention (e.g., rayḥān al-ḥamāḥim). How does this affect the ruling?
Local custom determines what is considered rayḥān in a given region.
5
Apply the ruling
Given the oath, if the person smells rose, what is the consequence?
No kaffārah is required; the oath remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يشم ريحاناً لا يحنث بشم ورد وياسمين لأن الريحان عند الفقهاء ما لساقه رائحة طيبة كما لو رقه . وقيل في عرف أهل العراق اسم لما لا ساق له من البقول مما له رائحة مستلذة. وقيل اسم لما ليس له شجر. وعلى كل فليس الورد والياسمين منه وإن كان في اللغة اسم لكل ما طاب ريحه من النبات. وفي فتح القدير والذي يجب أن يعول عليه في ديارنا إهدار ذلك كله لأن الريحان متعارف لنوع وهو ريحان الحماحم ، وأما الريحان الترنجي منه فيمكن أن لا يكون لأنهم
His statement: 'He does not smell rayḥān; he does not break the oath by smelling rose and jasmine' because rayḥān according to the jurists is that whose stem has a pleasant scent, as if it has leaves. And it is said in the custom of the people of Iraq that it is a name for what has no stem from the herbs that have a pleasant scent. And it is said it is a name for what has no tree. In any case, rose and jasmine are not from it, although in language it is a name for every plant that has a pleasant scent. And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: 'What must be relied upon in our lands is to disregard all that, because rayḥān is conventionally known as a type, which is rayḥān al-ḥamāḥim. As for rayḥān at-turunji, it may not be because they...'
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on an oath where a person swears not to smell 'rayḥān' (sweet basil). The commentator explains that the juristic definition of rayḥān is specific: it refers to plants that have a stem with a pleasant scent, such as basil. Roses and jasmine are not included because they do not have the characteristic stem. The text mentions different opinions on the definition of rayḥān according to the custom of the people of Iraq, but ultimately, roses and jasmine are excluded. The commentator cites Fatḥ al-Qadīr, which states that in their region, the conventional meaning of rayḥān is a specific type (ḥamāḥim), and the turunji type may not be included. Therefore, the oath is not broken by smelling rose or jasmine.

Key Terms
الريحان (ar-rayḥān): A type of plant with a stem and pleasant scent, specifically identified by custom; does not include roses or jasmine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He breaks the oath because rose has a pleasant scent.
B) He does not break the oath because rose is not considered rayḥān in the juristic sense.
C) He breaks the oath only if the rose is locally called rayḥān.
D) He must perform kaffārah for smelling any sweet plant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the term 'rayḥān' is interpreted according to juristic convention, not general linguistic usage, so roses and jasmine do not violate an oath not to smell rayḥān.

Oath Not to Smell Rayhan: Custom Defines Its Scope
Defining the meaning of 'rayhan' in oaths using custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'urf to determine the scope of an ambiguous term in an oath and state when the oath is broken or not.

Core Idea
Custom, not dictionary, decides what 'rayhan' means in an oath.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq demonstrates how the Hanafi school uses customary usage ('urf) to interpret ambiguous terms in oaths. The author clarifies that rose and jasmine are not considered 'rayhan' by custom, so an oath not to smell rayhan is not broken by them. He also discusses the pronunciation of the verb and the requirement of intentionality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Locate the term 'rayhan' in the oath and note that it has multiple possible meanings: linguistic, juristic, and customary.
The term is the core of the dispute.
2
Understand the linguistic definition
Recognize that linguistically, rayhan includes any fragrant plant, which would include rose and jasmine.
This is the default dictionary meaning.
3
See how custom overrides
Observe that the Hanafi school gives priority to the customary definition ('urf) over the linguistic one. In Iraq and Egypt, custom restricts rayhan to a specific type (hamahim).
Custom varies by region.
4
Apply to the ruling
Conclude that an oath not to smell rayhan is only broken by intentionally smelling the custom-defined type, not rose or jasmine.
Intentionality is also required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يشم ريحاناً لا يحنث بشم ورد وياسمين لأن الريحان عند الفقهاء ما لساقه رائحة طيبة كما لو رقه . وقيل في عرف أهل العراق اسم لما لا ساق له من البقول مما له رائحة مستلذة. وقيل اسم لما ليس له شجر. وعلى كل فليس الورد والياسمين منه وإن كان في اللغة اسم لكل ما طاب ريحه من النبات. وفي فتح القدير والذي يجب أن يعول عليه في ديارنا إهدار ذلك كله لأن الريحان متعارف لنوع وهو ريحان الحماحم ، وأما الريحان الترنجي منه فيمكن أن لا يكون لأنهم يلزمونه التقييد فيقال ريحان ترنجي وعندنا يطلقون اسم الريحان لا يفهم منه إلا الحماحم فلا يحنث إلا بعين ذلك النوع اهـ. وما قاله الواقع في مصر. ويشم - بفتح الياء والشين . مضارع شممت الطيب بكسر الميم في الماضي. هذه هي اللغة المشهورة الفصيحة، وأما شممته أشمه بفتح الميم في الماضي وضمها في المضارع فقد أنكرها بعض أهل اللغة وقال: خطأ. هو وصحح عدمه فقد نقلها الفراء وغيره وإن كانت ليست بفصيحة ثم يمين الشم تنعقد على الشم المقصود. فلو حلف لا يشم طيباً فوجد ريحه لم يحنث ولو وصلت الرائحة إلى دماغه كذا في فتح القدير .
He said: (He does not smell rayhan, he does not break his oath by smelling rose and jasmine because rayhan according to the jurists is what has a pleasant scent in its stalk, like its leaf. And it was said in the custom of the people of Iraq it is a name for what has no stalk among herbs that have a pleasant scent. And it was said a name for what has no tree. In any case, rose and jasmine are not from it even if in language it is a name for every plant with a pleasant scent. And in Fath al-Qadir: what should be relied upon in our lands is to disregard all that because rayhan is commonly known for a type, which is rayhan al-hamahim. As for rayhan al-turunji among it, it may not be because they require qualification and it is said turunji rayhan, and among us when they utter the name rayhan, nothing is understood except al-hamahim, so one does not break his oath except by that specific type. End. And what he said is the reality in Egypt. And yashummu - with fath of the ya and shin, present tense of shamimtu al-tib with kasr of the mim in the past. This is the well-known eloquent language. As for shamimtuhu ashummu with fath of the mim in the past and damm in the present, some linguists have rejected it and said it is wrong. He confirmed its incorrectness, but al-Farra' and others have transmitted it even if it is not eloquent. Then the oath of smelling is concluded on intentional smelling. So if one swears not to smell perfume and finds its scent, he does not break his oath, even if the smell reaches his brain. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the definition of rayhan (sweet basil) for the purpose of an oath. The matn states that if one swears not to smell rayhan, he does not break his oath by smelling rose or jasmine because, according to the fuqaha', rayhan is a plant with a fragrant stem, and rose and jasmine do not fall under this definition. However, since definitions vary by region, the author cites Fath al-Qadir to rely on the custom of the local area — in Egypt, rayhan is commonly understood to mean a specific type (hamahim), not rose or jasmine. The passage also clarifies the correct pronunciation of 'yashummu' and notes that the oath is only broken by intentional smelling, not by merely encountering a scent. This reflects the Hanafi reliance on 'urf in interpreting ambiguous terms in oaths.

Key Terms
ريحان (rayhan): In this text, specifically a type of sweet basil (hamahim) determined by local custom.
عرف (urf): Custom of the people, which overrides literal linguistic meaning in determining the scope of an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because jasmine is a type of fragrant plant.
B) No, because the custom determines that rayhan does not include jasmine.
C) Yes, if the jasmine has a stem.
D) No, only if he smells it intentionally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the scope of an ambiguous term in an oath is determined by local custom, not by dictionary definitions.

Oaths and Custom: Violet Leaves vs Oil
Ruling on whether buying leaves or oil of violet/rose breaks an oath, based on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an oath to avoid a named item is broken based on local custom rather than literal meaning.

Core Idea
Custom determines the referent of ambiguous terms in oaths.

When someone swears not to buy 'violet', does that include the leaves, the oil, or both? The answer is not found in dictionaries but in the custom of the community. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates how Hanafi jurists rely on 'urf to interpret ambiguous terms in oaths, leading to different rulings in different regions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is an oath not to buy violet or rose. Identify the ruling from the base text: buying leaves breaks the oath, buying oil does not.
This is the default ruling based on the author's custom.
2
Locate the basis: custom
The passage states that 'they apply to the leaf not the oil in our custom.' The ruling hinges on what the community means by 'violet' or 'rose'.
Custom (urf) is the operative principle.
3
Note the disagreement between regions
Al-Mabsut records a contrary view: buying leaf does NOT break, but buying oil does, because in that custom 'violet' refers to oil. Al-Karkhi then reports Baghdad custom where both break.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school, based on different customs.
4
Determine the operative ruling
The mufta bihi is that the ruling depends on the specific local custom at the time and place of the oath. No single universal rule applies.
The judge or mufti must ascertain the local 'urf.
5
Apply the principle to other cases
The passage extends the same reasoning to jasmine (its oil is called zanbaq, not jasmine) and henna (which refers to the powder in some customs).
Use analogy: the name determines the coverage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (البنفسج والورد على الورق فلو حلف لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً فاشترى ورقهما يحنث ولو اشترى دهنهما لا يحنث لأنهما يقعان على الورق دون الدهن في عرفنا ؛ كذا في الكافي وفي المبسوط : لو اشترى ورق البنفسج لا يحنث، ولو اشترى دهنه يحنث لأن اسم البنفسج إذا أطلق يراد به الدهن ويسمى بائعه بائع البنفسج فيصير هو بشرائه مشتري البنفسج أيضاً وهو رواية الجامع الصغير. وذكر الكرخي أنه يحنث بالورق كالدهن وهذا شيء يبتني على العرف وفي عرف أهل الكوفة بائع الورق لا بائع البنفسج وإنما يسمى بائع الدهن فبني الجواب في الكتاب على ذلك. ثم شاهد الكرخي عرف أهل بغداد أنهم يسمون بائع الورق بائع البنفسج أيضاً فقال : يحنث به. وقال: هكذا في ديارنا أعني في المبسوط ولا يقال في أحدهما حقيقة وفي الآخر مجازاً بل فيهما حقيقة ويحنث فيهما باعتبار عموم المجاز، والياسمين قياس الورد لا يتناول الدهن لأن دهنه يسمى زنبقاً لا ياسميناً، وكذا الحناء يتناول الورق هذا إذا لم تكن له نية. وقال في الكافي: الحناء تقع في عرفنا على المدقوق
He said: (Violet and rose are on the leaf, so if he swears not to buy violet or rose and buys their leaves, he breaks his oath, and if he buys their oil, he does not break his oath, because they apply to the leaf not the oil in our custom. Thus in al-Kafi. And in al-Mabsut: If he buys the leaf of violet, he does not break his oath, and if he buys its oil, he breaks his oath because the name violet when used absolutely refers to the oil and its seller is called a seller of violet, so he by buying it becomes a buyer of violet as well. This is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. And al-Karkhi mentioned that he breaks his oath with the leaf as with oil, and this is built on custom. In the custom of the people of Kufa, the seller of leaf is not called a seller of violet, only the seller of oil, so the answer in the book was built on that. Then al-Karkhi witnessed the custom of the people of Baghdad that they call the seller of leaf a seller of violet as well, so he said: He breaks his oath with it. And he said: Thus in our lands meaning in al-Mabsut. And it is not said that one is real and the other metaphorical, rather both are real and he breaks his oath by both due to the generality of the metaphor. Jasmine by analogy to rose does not include oil because its oil is called zanbaq not jasmine. Likewise, henna includes leaf when he has no intention, and in al-Kafi: Henna in our custom applies to the ground powder.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on oaths not to buy violet (banafsaj) or rose (ward). The matn states that if one swears not to buy violet or rose and then buys their leaves, he breaks his oath, but if he buys their oil, he does not. The commentator explains that this is based on the custom of the place: in some areas, the name 'violet' applies to the leaf, while in others, it applies to the oil. Al-Karkhi noted that in Kufa, only the oil is called violet, so buying the leaf does not break the oath; in Baghdad, the leaf is also called violet, so buying it breaks the oath. The final ruling depends on the local custom. Jasmine oil is called zanbaq, so it does not fall under jasmine. Henna also depends on custom; in some places, henna refers to the powder, not the leaf. The passage highlights that oaths are interpreted based on the custom of the community at the time of the oath.

Key Terms
بنفسج (banafsaj): In this text, the name used for violet; whether it applies to the leaf or the oil depends on custom.
عرف (urf): Custom that determines what is meant by a term in a specific locality.
زنبق (zanbaq): The name for jasmine oil, distinguishing it from the flower itself.
حناء (hinna'): Henna; in some customs, it refers to the ground powder, not the leaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following determines whether buying the leaf of violet breaks an oath not to buy violet?
A) The seller's intention
B) The local custom
C) The dictionary definition
D) The cost of the item

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of ambiguous terms in oaths is based on the local custom (urf) at the time and place of the oath.

Oath of Having No Property: Debt Owed Does Not Count
Ruling that debt is not property for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a debt owed to the oath-taker does not constitute property for the purpose of an oath and apply this ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
A debt owed is not property; an oath about lacking property remains unbroken even if one is owed money.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are taken seriously, and breaking an oath requires expiation. This lesson introduces a specific ruling: when a person swears that he has no property, a debt owed to him by someone else does not break his oath, regardless of the debtor's solvency. This distinction between tangible property and receivables is key to understanding many oath-related rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The person swears that he has no property (mal). This is a common oath regarding one's financial state.
2
Identify the existence of a debt
The oath-taker is owed a debt by another person, whether the debtor is insolvent (muflis) or solvent (mali'). The debt is a claim, not tangible property.
3
Apply the ruling
The oath is not broken because the debt is not considered property in his possession for the purpose of the oath.
This is independent of the debtor's ability to pay.
4
Understand the basis
The Hanafi definition of mal (property) for oaths refers to tangible assets in hand (al-‘ayn), not claims or debts (al-dayn). This is a technical distinction.
This ruling is taken from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sharh Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حلف بأنه لا مال له وله دين على مفلس أو مليء لا يحنث
He swore that he has no property and he has a debt owed to him by an insolvent or solvent person; he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states a clear ruling: if a person swears that he has no property (mal) while he is owed a debt by someone, whether the debtor is insolvent (muflis) or solvent (mali'), the oath is not broken. This is because a debt is not considered actual property in his possession for the purpose of the oath; it is a mere claim. The ruling is based on the Hanafi position that 'mal' in oaths refers to tangible assets in one's possession, not receivables. This is a straightforward ruling for memorization.

Key Terms
مال (mal): Property; in this context, tangible assets under one's possession, not debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears that he has no property, but he is owed a debt of $1000 by a solvent friend, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the debt is a valuable asset.
B) No, because debts are not considered property in oaths.
C) Yes, if the friend is solvent and can pay.
D) No, only if the friend is insolvent.

Key Takeaway
A debt owed to someone does not count as property for the purpose of an oath; thus an oath about lacking property is not broken by having receivables.

Oaths for Violets and Roses: Custom Determines Meaning
Expansion probe on oath interpretation based on urf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how local custom (urf) determines the scope of an oath regarding specific plants, and apply this principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
The meaning of a plant name in oaths depends on local custom, not a universal definition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq illustrates a key principle in Hanafi oath rulings: the interpretation of a swearer's words is governed by the customary understanding in their locale. The specific case of violets and roses shows that different Hanafi authorities gave different rulings based on the customs of Kufa and Baghdad. The text then extends the same logic to jasmine and henna.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
A person swears not to buy violets or roses. Does buying the leaves or the oil of these plants break the oath? The answer hinges on what the name 'violet' or 'rose' means in the swearer's custom.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Three positions are reported: (1) al-Kafi: buying leaves breaks the oath, oil does not. (2) al-Mabsut & al-Jami' al-Saghir: leaves do not break the oath, oil does. (3) al-Karkhi: both break the oath based on Baghdad custom.
The disagreement arises from different regional customs.
3
Understand the rationale: custom as the basis
Each view is explicitly based on what the term 'violet' commonly refers to in the scholar's locale. The text states that both uses are literal in their respective customs, not one literal and one metaphorical.
The ruling is not arbitrary; it follows established Hanafi principles of interpreting oaths by urf.
4
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The passage extends the analysis to jasmine and henna. For jasmine, oil is not included because it is called 'zanbaq', not jasmine. For henna, the leaves are included; in some regions henna refers to the powder. This shows the principle is general.
The qiyas is based on the same illa: the name of the plant versus the name of its derivative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (البنفسج والورد على الورق فلو حلف لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو ورداً فاشترى ورقهما يحنث ولو اشترى دهنهما لا يحنث لأنهما يقعان على الورق دون الدهن في عرفنا؛ كذا في الكافي وفي المبسوط: لو اشترى ورق البنفسج لا يحنث، ولو اشترى دهنه يحنث لأن اسم البنفسج إذا أطلق يراد به الدهن ويسمى بائعه بائع البنفسج فيصير هو بشرائه مشتري البنفسج أيضاً وهو رواية الجامع الصغير. وذكر الكرخي أنه يحنث بالورق كالدهن وهذا شيء يبتني على العرف وفي عرف أهل الكوفة بائع الورق لا يسمى بائع البنفسج وإنما يسمى بائع الدهن فبني الجواب في الكتاب على ذلك. ثم شاهد الكرخي عرف أهل بغداد أنهم يسمون بائع الورق بائع البنفسج أيضاً فقال: يحنث به. وقال: هكذا في ديارنا أعني في المبسوط ولا يقال في أحدهما حقيقة وفي الآخر مجازاً بل فيهما حقيقة ويحنث فيهما باعتبار عموم المجاز، والياسمين قياس الورد لا يتناول الدهن لأن دهنه يسمى زنبقاً لا ياسميناً، وكذا الحناء يتناول الورق هذا إذا لم تكن له نية. وقال في الكافي: الحناء تقع في عرفنا على المدقوق
His statement (regarding violets and roses concerning the leaves): So if he swears not to buy a violet or a rose, and he buys their leaves, he breaks the oath; but if he buys their oil, he does not break it, because in our custom they refer to the leaves, not the oil. Thus in al-Kafi. In al-Mabsut: If he buys violet leaves, he does not break the oath; if he buys its oil, he breaks it, because when 'violet' is uttered absolutely, the oil is intended, and its seller is called a violet-seller, so by buying it he becomes a buyer of violet as well. This is the transmission from al-Jami' al-Saghir. Al-Karkhi said that he breaks the oath with leaves as with oil. This is based on custom. In the custom of the people of Kufa, a seller of leaves is not called a violet-seller; only the seller of oil is so called. Thus the answer in the book is built on that. Then al-Karkhi observed the custom of the people of Baghdad; they call a seller of leaves also a violet-seller, so he said he breaks the oath with it. He said: thus in our regions, I mean in al-Mabsut; and it is not said that in one it is literal and in the other metaphorical, but both are literal, and he breaks the oath in both due to the generality of the metaphor. Jasmine is analogous to roses; it does not include oil because its oil is called 'zanbaq', not jasmine. Similarly, henna includes the leaves; this is when there is no intention. In al-Kafi: Henna in our custom refers to the powdered form.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling for oaths regarding violets (بنفسج) and roses (ورد). The basic ruling from al-Kafi is that if one swears not to buy violets or roses, buying the leaves breaks the oath, but buying the oil does not, because in the author's custom 'violet' refers to the leaves. However, al-Mabsut reports a different ruling based on a riwayah from al-Jami' al-Saghir: buying violet leaves does not break the oath, but buying its oil does, because in Kufa 'violet' commonly means the oil. Al-Karkhi adds that in Baghdad, even leaves are called violets, so buying leaves also breaks the oath. The passage emphasizes that the ruling hinges on local custom (urf). It then extends the rule to jasmine (ياسمين): buying its oil does not break the oath because the oil is called 'zanbaq', not jasmine. For henna (حناء), the leaves are included unless otherwise intended, and in some regions henna refers to the powdered form. The key principle: if the swearer had a specific intention (نية), that governs; otherwise, custom decides.

Key Terms
بنفسج (banafsaj): Violet flower; in the text, the term can refer to the leaves or the oil depending on custom.
ورد (ward): Rose; similar to violet, the term's scope depends on custom.
دهن (duhn): Oil extracted from the flower.
ورق (waraq): Leaves of the plant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Karkhi's observation, buying violet leaves in Baghdad (where sellers of leaves are called violet-sellers) breaks the oath. True or false?
A) True
B) False

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the meaning of a plant name is determined by local custom, and the same principle extends to analogous cases like jasmine and henna.

Definition of a House in Oaths: Ownership vs. Dwelling
Definition of house in oaths based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what constitutes entering a house in oaths, distinguish between ownership and dwelling, and apply it to scenarios involving owned, rented, or shared houses.

Core Idea
In an oath, 'house' means the dwelling place, not merely owned property — custom (urf) governs interpretation.

When a person swears not to enter someone's house, what counts as 'house'? Is it any property they own, or only where they actually live? This lesson examines a key passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifying that the oath is broken only by entering the dwelling place, whether owned, rented, or borrowed. We'll explore the Hanafi reliance on custom and see how this ruling plays out in different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is entering a dwelling after swearing not to enter a specific person's house. The ruling is that the oath is broken if the dwelling belongs to that person by ownership, tenancy, or borrowing.
The key is that 'dwelling' is defined by custom.
2
Determine the criterion
The criterion is the customary meaning of 'house' (dar). In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted according to urf (custom). Customarily, a house is the place where one dwells, not merely a property one owns.
This is based on the principle that the literal meaning is not controlling when custom gives a different implication.
3
Distinguish between ownership and dwelling
If the person owns the house but does not dwell there (e.g., it is rented out or vacant), entering it does not break the oath. The oath only applies to the dwelling place.
This distinction is crucial in practice.
4
Apply to scenarios
Apply the rule: (a) owned and lived in → breaks oath; (b) owned but not lived in → does not break; (c) rented or borrowed dwelling → breaks oath because it is the dwelling; (d) shared house where the person dwells with others → breaks oath even if he owns only part.
The passage also discusses marriage oaths, which are not part of this lesson.
5
Note the role of intention (niyyah)
The passage from al-Waqi'at indicates that if the swearer had a specific intention (e.g., only owned houses), he is believed in his intention. Otherwise, custom prevails.
This is a standard Hanafi rule: in oaths, the intention of the swearer is respected if plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وداره بالملك والإجارة) أي لو حلف يدخل دار فلان يحنث بدخول ما يسكنه بالملك والإجارة لأن المراد به المسكن عرفاً فدخل ما يسكنه بأي سبب كان بإجارة أو إعارة أو ملك باعتبار عموم المجاز. ومعناه أن يكون محل الحقيقة فرداً من أفراد المجاز لا باعتبار الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. قيدنا بأن تكون مسكنه لأنه لو لم يكن ساكناً فيها وهي ملكه لا يحنث قال في الواقعات حلف لا يدخل دار فلان يحتاج إليها الخ أنه لا حاجة إلى قوله ويجيز بالفعل إذ لا فرق يظهر بين تدخل في عصمتي وبين تدخل في نكاحي أو تصير حلالاً لي، وقد تقدم عن الخلاصة أن هذين بمنزلة كل امرأة أتزوجها. ثم ظهر لي الجواب وهو أن قوله ( وهذه الحيلة الخ) بالنظر إلى قوله أو يزوجها غيري لأجلي» وظاهر أن تزويج الغير يوجد بدون الإجازة قولاً أو فعلاً، أما لو اقتصر على قوله أتزوجها فلا بد من الإجازة فيما لو زوجه الفضولي لأنه لا يوجد تزوجه بدونها. ومثله قوله تدخل في عصمتين فإنه مثل أتزوجها لا مثل يزوجها غيري لأجلي فإنه بتزويج الفضولي لا تدخل في عصمته بل هو موقوف على إجارته قوله : (وفي القنية إن تزوجت عليك الخ هذا مخالف لما مر عن الخلاصة من التسوية بين «أتزوجها» وبين «تدخل في نكاحي فتأمل قوله : ( فلا مخلص له إلا إذا كان المعلق طلاق المتزوجة فيرفع الأمر إلى شافعي) أقول : ما مر عن الفصولين عدم ا الحاجة إلى الرفع إلى الشافعي بأن يزوجه فضولي بلا أمرهما فيجيزه لأن قوله «أو أجزت نكاح فضولي ولو بالفعل لا يزيد على قوله أو يزوجها غيرها لأجلي وأجيزه» تأمل . إلا أن يقال بناء على القول الأول في المسألة المارة وهو أنه لا وجه الجوازه تأمل قول المصنف : (وداره بالملك والإجارة) قال الرملي : قدم في شرح قوله والواقف على السطح داخل» عن المجتبى : لو قال إن دخلت دار زيد فعبدي حر وإن دخلت دار عمرو فامرأتي طالق فدخل دار زيد وهي في يد عمرو بإجارة يعتق وتطلق إذا لم ينو فإن نوى شيئاً صدق ا.هـ. وبه علم أنه إذا نوى الملك هنا خاصة يصدق وهي واقعة الفتوى قوله : (قيدنا بأن تكون مسكنه قال الرملي : قدم في شرح قوله «وإن جعلت بستاناً أو حماماً الخ لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان لو دخل داراً مملوكه لفلان وفلان لا يسكنها يحنث، فيحمل ما فدخل داراً بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة أو غيرها . وأطلق في الملك فشمل الدار المشتركة، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها يحنث لأن جميع الدار تصاف إليه بعضها بالملك وكلها بالسكني، ولا بد أن يكون سكنى فلان بها لا بطريق التبعية، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلانة فدخل دارها وزوجها ساكن فيها لا يحنث لأن الدار تنسب إلى الساكن والساكن هو الزوج، كذا في الواقعات وقد قدمناها في بحث الدخول
He said: (And his house by ownership and tenancy) i.e., if he swears to enter the house of so-and-so, he breaks the oath by entering what he dwells in by ownership or tenancy, because what is meant by it is the dwelling place customarily. So it includes what he dwells in by any cause, whether by tenancy, borrowing, or ownership, based on the generality of metonymy. Its meaning is that the place of reality is an individual of the individuals of metonymy, not based on combining literal and metonymic. We restricted it to being his dwelling because if he is not dwelling in it and it is his property, he does not break the oath. It is stated in al-Waqi'at: He swore not to enter the house of so-and-so... etc.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage explains the scope of the term 'house' in an oath. The key principle is that the house is understood customarily as the place of dwelling, regardless of whether it is owned, rented, or borrowed. Therefore, entering a dwelling place owned by the person breaks the oath, but entering a property he owns but does not reside in does not. The commentator also discusses the distinction between literal and metonymic usage, and includes a side discussion about the marriage oath, which is part of the commentary flow.

Key Terms
دار (dār): A house; in the context of oaths, it includes any place of dwelling.
مسكن (maskan): Dwelling place; the place where one resides.
عرف (urf): Custom; a source of law in Hanafi fiqh, used to interpret ambiguous terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman swears not to enter her sister's house, and the sister lives in a house owned by her husband, does entering that house break the oath?
A) Yes, because she dwells there.
B) No, because she is not the owner.
C) No, because the house belongs to the husband.
D) Yes, because she is married to the owner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath about entering a house is interpreted according to custom, so only entering the dwelling place breaks the oath, not entering owned but uninhabited property.

Oaths on Entering Shared and Daughters' Houses
Critical refinement of ruling on oaths with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on entering a shared house or a daughter's house under oath, and APPLY the distinction between a dwelling house and a rental house to determine if the oath is broken.

Core Idea
The oath is broken only if the house entered is attributed to the person named, considering the dweller and the swearer's intention.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses nuanced cases in oaths about entering houses, beginning with a textual correction and then exploring scenarios of shared ownership, rental properties, and daughters living in their husbands' homes. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying rulings on oath violations (hanath). We will examine the proofs from various Hanafi sources and the reconciliation of differing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual error
The commentator notes that in some copies the phrase 'does not break the oath' appears, but the correct reading is 'breaks the oath' when the house is vacant and still attributed to the person. This is based on the apparent context and preceding text.
Textual criticism in fiqh works is important to ascertain the correct ruling.
2
Apply the rule for shared houses
If a person swears not to enter the house of so-and-so, and enters a house shared between them (where so-and-so dwells), the oath is broken because the house is attributed to that person due to his dwelling.
Dwelling, not ownership, is the primary factor for attribution.
3
Distinguish between dwelling and rental house
If the person has a rental house (dar ghallah) he does not live in, entering it does not break the oath unless there is evidence that the swearer intended that house. The default attribution is to the dwelling house.
Rental houses are not considered 'his house' in the context of oaths unless specifically intended.
4
Evaluate the daughter's house case
There is a conflict: al-Khaniyyah says entering the husband's house breaks the oath; al-Waqi'at says no. The commentator reconciles by considering whether the daughter owns the house and the swearer's intention.
The reconciliation shows that the ruling depends on ownership and customary attribution.
5
Reconcile with additional criteria
Al-Fadli adds: if the daughter has no other house attributed to her, the oath is broken; otherwise, not. This provides another basis for resolving the ikhtilaf.
Intra-Hanafi differences are resolved by considering all relevant factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هنا على ما إذا كانت مسكونة لغيره، أما إذا كانت حالية فيحنث إذا لم تنقطع نسبتها عنه وإضافتها إليه تأمل قوله : (لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة كذا في النسخ والصواب حذف «لا» من قوله «لا يحنث» كما يدل عليه الظاهر والسباق والسياق وقد ذكر المسألة قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس حيث قال عازياً إلى الظهيرية: ولو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بينه وبين فلان، إن كان فلان يسكنها يحنث وإلا فلا. وذكر قبلها عازياً إلى المحيط : لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان وله دار يسكنها ودار غلة فدخل دار الغلة لا يحنث إذا لم يدل الدليل على دار الغلة وغيرها لأن داره مطلقاً دار يسكنها قوله : (كذا في الواقعات) أقول : يخالفه ما مر قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس الخ» معزياً إلى الخانية : لو حلف لا يدخل دار ابنته وابنته تسكن في دار زوجها أو حلف لا يدخل دار أمه وأمه تسكن في بيت زوجها فدخل الحالف حنث ا هـ . وكذا ذكر في النهر عند قول المتن وفي طاق الباب لا ما نصه : ولا فرق في الساكن بين ركوبه تبعاً أو حتى لو حلف إلى آخر ما ذكره في الخانية لكن ذكر في الخانية قبل هذه المسألة بنحو ورقتين عين الفرع المنقول هنا عن الواقعات. وقال في جوابه: إن لم ينو تلك الدار لا يحنث لأن السكني تضاف إلى الزوج لا إلى المرأة، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن الدار في المسألة المارة لما لم تكن ملكاً للمرأة أريدت السكنى بطريق التبعية، ولما كانت الدار في مسألتنا ملكاً لها انعقدت اليمين على السكني بالإصالة، ولما كان زوجها ساكناً معها صارت تبعاً له لأنها تضاف حينئذ إلى الزوج فلم يوجد شرط الحنث لكن رأيت في التتارخانية ما يفيد اختلاف الرواية حيث ذكر مسألة الواقعات ثم ذكر الثانية عن المنتقى ثم قال : وهذه الرواية تخالف ما ذكر . ثم ذكر عن الفضلي تفصيلاً وهو أنه إن لم يكن للمحلوف عليه دار أخرى ننسب إليه يحنث وإلا فلا. قال : ولم يذكر هذا في المنتقى ا.هـ. وفي البزازية بعد ذكره التفصيل المذكور قال : وفي المنتقى اختار الحنث مطلقاً اعتباراً بالمساكنة إلا إذا نوى داراً مملوكة لكل منهما ا هـ. والله سبحانه أعلم.
Here regarding when it is inhabited by someone else. If it is vacant, then he breaks the oath if its attribution to him is not cut off and its addition to him. Contemplate his saying: (He does not break the oath unless there is evidence for the rental house) as in the copies, but the correct is to delete 'la' from his saying 'he does not break the oath' as indicated by the apparent context and the preceding and following text. And he mentioned the issue before his saying 'And continuous riding and wearing' where he said, citing al-Zahiriyyah: If he swears not to enter the house of so-and-so, and he enters a shared house between him and so-and-so, if so-and-so dwells in it, he breaks the oath; otherwise, not. And before that he cited from al-Muhit: If he swears not to enter the house of so-and-so and he has a house he dwells in and a rental house, he enters the rental house, he does not break the oath if there is no evidence for the rental house or other, because his house absolutely is the house he dwells in. He said: (Thus in al-Waqi'at) I say: It contradicts what came before his saying 'And continuous riding and wearing' citing al-Khaniyyah: If he swears not to enter the house of his daughter and his daughter dwells in the house of her husband, or he swears not to enter the house of his mother and his mother dwells in the house of her husband, and the swearer enters, he breaks the oath... etc.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage addresses specific cases regarding oaths about entering houses. The commentator first corrects a textual error: a copy says 'he does not break the oath' but the correct version is 'he breaks the oath' when the house is vacant and still attributed to the person. Then it discusses the case of a shared house: if the person swears not to enter the house of so-and-so, and so-and-so lives in a shared house, entering that house breaks the oath. If he has a rental house that he does not live in, entering it does not break the oath unless there is evidence that he meant that house. The passage also tackles the case of a daughter living with her husband: if a father swears not to enter his daughter's house, does he break the oath by entering the husband's house? There is a difference of opinion. Some say he breaks the oath because the daughter resides there, others say no because the house is attributed to the husband. The commentator reconciles by distinguishing whether the daughter owns the house or not. Finally, it mentions that according to al-Fadli, if the person has no other house attributed to him, he breaks the oath; otherwise, not. The passage highlights the importance of intention and custom.

Key Terms
دار غلة (dār ghallah): A rental house; a house from which one derives income, not for dwelling.
حنث (ḥanath): Breaking an oath; committing a violation of the oath that requires kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct ruling when someone swears not to enter the house of a person who dwells in a shared house?
A) He does not break the oath because the house is shared.
B) He breaks the oath if that person dwells there.
C) He breaks the oath only if he owns the house.
D) He does not break the oath unless the house is solely owned by that person.

Key Takeaway
The determination of breaking an oath about entering a house hinges on the customary attribution of the house to the person, the dweller's status, and the swearer's intention, with no blanket rule.
