Ruling on Oaths Not to Enter a Shared House
Ruling on oath-breaking for shared houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking an oath not to enter a shared house and identify the key condition—residency versus ownership—and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Residency attributes the entire house; ownership alone does not break the oath.

This ruling clarifies a common confusion: is entering a jointly owned house a violation of an oath not to enter a specific person's house? The Hanafi position hinges on residency, not ownership. The passage also reveals a nuanced dispute among later authorities regarding dependent residency, such as a woman living with her husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath
Determine the exact wording: 'I will not enter so-and-so's house.' Note whether the house is solely owned or shared.
2
Check residency
Does so-and-so actually reside in the house? If yes, the oath is broken even if the house is jointly owned.
Residency is the decisive factor, not ownership.
3
Assess dependency
If so-and-so is a dependent (e.g., a woman living with her husband), the house is attributed to the resident (husband); the oath is not broken unless a specific intention.
The dependent's residency is not considered as 'sukna' independent.
4
Consider rental property
If the house is a rental property (dar al-ghullah) and so-and-so does not live there, the oath is not broken unless the oath explicitly includes that house.
The default is that a person's 'house' is the one they reside in.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Different sources: al-Waqi'at rules no breaking for a woman with husband; al-Khaniya rules breaking. The mufta bihi from al-Muntaqa is absolute breaking if co-residence, unless specific intention.
This affects whether expiation is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدخل داراً بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة أو غيرها . وأطلق في الملك فشمل الدار المشتركة، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها يحنث لأن جميع الدار تصاف إليه بعضها بالملك وكلها بالسكني، ولا بد أن يكون سكنى فلان بها لا بطريق التبعية، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلانة فدخل دارها وزوجها ساكن فيها لا يحنث لأن الدار تنسب إلى الساكن والساكن هو الزوج، كذا في الواقعات وقد قدمناها في بحث الدخول قوله : (حلف بأنه لا مال له وله دين على مفلس أو مليء لا يحنث لأن الدين ليس بمال وإنما هو وصف في الذمة لا يتصور قبضه حقيقة ولهذا قيل : إن الديون تقضى بأمثالها على معنى أن المقبوض مضمون على القابض لأنه قبضه لنفسه على وجه التملك ولرب الدين على المدين مثله فالتقى الدينان قصاصاً فصار غيره حقيقة وشرعاً؛ أما الحقيقة فظاهر، وأما الشرع فلا حاجة إلى اسقاط اعتباره لأن التصرف في الدين قبل القبض جائز والمفلس بالتشديد رجل حكم القاضي بإفلاسه والمليء الغني؛ ذكره مسكين والله أعلم . تم الجزء الرابع من البحر ويليه الجزء الخامس وأوله كتاب الحدود
هنا على ما إذا كانت مسكونة لغيره، أما إذا كانت حالية فيحنث إذا لم تنقطع نسبتها عنه وإضافتها إليه تأمل قوله : (لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة كذا في النسخ والصواب حذف «لا» من قوله «لا يحنث» كما يدل عليه الظاهر والسباق والسياق وقد ذكر المسألة قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس حيث قال عازياً إلى الظهيرية: ولو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بينه وبين فلان، إن كان فلان يسكنها يحنث وإلا فلا. وذكر قبلها عازياً إلى المحيط : لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان وله دار يسكنها ودار غلة فدخل دار الغلة لا يحنث إذا لم يدل الدليل على دار الغلة وغيرها لأن داره مطلقاً دار يسكنها قوله : (كذا في الواقعات) أقول : يخالفه ما مر قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس الخ» معزياً إلى الخانية : لو حلف لا يدخل دار ابنته وابنته تسكن في دار زوجها أو حلف لا يدخل دار أمه وأمه تسكن في بيت زوجها فدخل الحالف حنث ا هـ . وكذا ذكر في النهر عند قول المتن وفي طاق الباب لا ما نصه : ولا فرق في الساكن بين ركوبه تبعاً أو حتى لو حلف إلى آخر ما ذكره في الخانية لكن ذكر في الخانية قبل هذه المسألة بنحو ورقتين عين الفرع المنقول هنا عن الواقعات. وقال في جوابه: إن لم ينو تلك الدار لا يحنث لأن السكني تضاف إلى الزوج لا إلى المرأة، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن الدار في المسألة المارة لما لم تكن ملكاً للمرأة أريدت السكنى بطريق التبعية، ولما كانت الدار في مسألتنا ملكاً لها انعقدت اليمين على السكني بالإصالة، ولما كان زوجها ساكناً معها صارت تبعاً له لأنها تضاف حينئذ إلى الزوج فلم يوجد شرط الحنث لكن رأيت في التتارخانية ما يفيد اختلاف الرواية حيث ذكر مسألة الواقعات ثم ذكر الثانية عن المنتقى ثم قال : وهذه الرواية تخالف ما ذكر . ثم ذكر عن الفضلي تفصيلاً وهو أنه إن لم يكن للمحلوف عليه دار أخرى ننسب إليه يحنث وإلا فلا. قال : ولم يذكر هذا في المنتقى ا.هـ. وفي البزازية بعد ذكره التفصيل المذكور قال : وفي المنتقى اختار الحنث مطلقاً اعتباراً بالمساكنة إلا إذا نوى داراً مملوكة لكل منهما ا هـ. والله سبحانه أعلم.
So he entered a house belonging to so-and-so and another, and so-and-so resides in it: he does not break his oath unless evidence indicates a rental property or other. And the text is absolute regarding ownership, so it includes a jointly owned house. Thus, if he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and enters a house jointly owned between so-and-so and another, and so-and-so resides in it, he breaks his oath because the entire house is attributed to him partly by ownership and wholly by residency. It is necessary that so-and-so's residency be not by way of dependency. Thus, if he swears not to enter so-and-so's house (a woman) and enters her house while her husband resides in it, he does not break his oath because the house is attributed to the resident, and the resident is the husband. This is as in al-Waqi'at, and we have presented it in the discussion of entry. His statement: (If he swears that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed by an insolvent or a wealthy person, he does not break his oath because a debt is not wealth; rather it is a description in the dhimmah that cannot actually be received. Thus it is said: Debts are repaid by their equivalents, meaning that what is received is a guarantee upon the receiver because he received it for himself by way of ownership, and the creditor has its equivalent against the debtor, so the two debts set off each other, becoming other than actual wealth both in reality and in law; as for reality, it is apparent; and as for law, there is no need to disregard it because transactions involving a debt before receipt are permissible. Al-Muflis (insolvent) stressed is a man whom the judge has declared insolvent, and al-mali' is the wealthy; mentioned by Miskin. And Allah knows best. The fourth volume of al-Bahr is completed, followed by the fifth volume, beginning with the Book of Hudud. Here regarding when it is inhabited by someone else, but if it is empty he breaks his oath if its attribution to him is not cut off and his addition to it. Consider his statement: (He does not break his oath unless evidence indicates a rental property) recorded in the copies, and the correct is to drop 'la' from his statement 'la yahanthu' as indicated by appearance, context, and sequence. He had mentioned the issue before his statement 'and continuity of riding and wearing' where he said, attributing to al-Zahiriya: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and enters a house jointly owned between him and so-and-so, if so-and-so resides in it he breaks his oath, otherwise no. And before it he mentioned, attributing to al-Muhit: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and he has a house he resides in and a rental house, then enters the rental house, he does not break his oath if evidence does not indicate the rental house or other, because his house absolutely is the one he resides in. His statement: (as in al-Waqi'at) I say: it is contradicted by what passed before his statement 'and continuity of riding and wearing' attributing to al-Khaniya: If he swears not to enter his daughter's house and his daughter resides in her husband's house, or swears not to enter his mother's house and his mother resides in her husband's house, then the swearer enters, he breaks his oath. And similar is mentioned in al-Nahr at the statement of the text 'and in the arch of the door, not what is stated: no difference in the resident between being a dependent or even if he swears...' until the rest of what al-Khaniya mentions. But al-Khaniya mentioned about two sheets before this issue the exact branch narrated here from al-Waqi'at. And he said in its answer: If he did not intend that house, he does not break his oath because residency is attributed to the husband, not to the woman. And it can be answered that the house in the previous issue, since it was not owned by the woman, residency was intended by way of dependency; and since the house in our issue is owned by her, the oath was made based on residency as original; and since her husband resides with her, she became dependent on him because then it is attributed to the husband, so the condition for breaking the oath is not met. But I saw in al-Tatarkhaniya something indicating a difference in narration: he mentioned the issue from al-Waqi'at, then mentioned the second from al-Muntaqa, then said: This narration contradicts what was mentioned. Then he mentioned from al-Fadli a detail: if the one sworn about has no other house to attribute to him, he breaks his oath; otherwise no. He said: This is not mentioned in al-Muntaqa. In al-Bazzaziya after mentioning the aforementioned detail, he said: In al-Muntaqa, he chose breaking the oath absolutely considering co-residence, unless he intended a house owned by each of them. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing two distinct rulings under the chapter of oaths concerning beating and killing. The first ruling deals with oaths not to enter a specific person's house. The author explains that if one swears not to enter 'so-and-so's house' and enters a house that is jointly owned by that person and another, the oath is broken only if so-and-so actually resides in that house. The reason is that the house is attributed to the resident, not just the owner. If the person does not live there (e.g., it is a rental property or empty), the oath is not broken. An exception is made for a woman: if one swears not to enter a woman's house and enters her house while her husband lives there, the oath is not broken because the house is attributed to the resident (the husband). The author then cites various sources (al-Waqi'at, al-Zahiriya, al-Muhit, al-Khaniya, al-Nahr, al-Tatarkhaniya, etc.) and discusses conflicting narrations, showing that there is ikhtilaf among later authorities. A detail from al-Fadli resolves the conflict by considering whether the person has another house to which they are attributed. The second ruling concerns oaths about having no wealth. If one swears that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed to him (whether from an insolvent or wealthy debtor), the oath is not broken because a debt is not considered wealth in the true sense; it is merely a description in the dhimma (liability) that cannot be physically received. The author explains that debts are repaid by equivalents, and upon receipt the creditor has a guarantee against the debtor, so the two debts offset. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that debts are not property for the purposes of oaths and zakat until actually received. The passage ends with a note about the completion of the fourth volume and the beginning of the fifth.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking of an oath, resulting in the obligation of expiation (kaffarah) unless the oath was made with a specific exception.
دار الغلة (dar al-ghullah): A house that generates rental income, as opposed to a house used for residence. The oath is not broken by entering such a house if the person does not live there.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to enter his mother's house, and his mother lives with her husband (his father), does he break his oath by entering?
A) Yes, because he entered her house.
B) No, because the house is attributed to the father as the resident.
C) Yes, because he violated his oath.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
For oaths about entering houses, the decisive factor is independent residency, not ownership.

Ruling on Oaths About Having No Wealth While Owed Debts
Ruling on debt and wealth in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a debt is not considered wealth for the purpose of oaths in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A debt is a description in the dhimma, not actual wealth; thus an oath of no wealth is not broken by having a debt.

This ruling addresses a common scenario: someone swears they have no wealth, but they are owed a debt. Is the oath broken? The Hanafi school rules it is not, because a debt is not considered property in the true sense. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath
The oath is 'I have no wealth (mal).' Understand what constitutes wealth in the legal context.
2
Define wealth legally
Wealth (mal) is generally understood as tangible property that can be possessed and utilized. Debts are not tangible.
Hanafis treat debts as a right in the dhimma, not as actual mal for oaths and zakat.
3
Classify the debt
A debt is a Dain (liability) that exists as an obligation in the dhimma. It cannot be physically received until payment.
The text says 'a debt is not wealth; it is a description in the dhimma'.
4
Apply the ruling
Since a debt is not considered wealth, the oath is not broken (لا يحنث). This holds whether the debtor is solvent or insolvent.
The text explicitly states: 'لا يحنث لأن الدين ليس بمال'.
5
Understand the rationale
The text explains that debts are repaid by equivalents; upon receipt, the creditor has a guarantee, not the actual thing. Thus, debts are not considered wealth for oaths.
This is based on the principle that transactions involving debts before receipt are permissible.

Source Text

فدخل داراً بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة أو غيرها . وأطلق في الملك فشمل الدار المشتركة، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بين فلان وغيره وفلان ساكنها يحنث لأن جميع الدار تصاف إليه بعضها بالملك وكلها بالسكني، ولا بد أن يكون سكنى فلان بها لا بطريق التبعية، فلو حلف لا يدخل دار فلانة فدخل دارها وزوجها ساكن فيها لا يحنث لأن الدار تنسب إلى الساكن والساكن هو الزوج، كذا في الواقعات وقد قدمناها في بحث الدخول قوله : (حلف بأنه لا مال له وله دين على مفلس أو مليء لا يحنث لأن الدين ليس بمال وإنما هو وصف في الذمة لا يتصور قبضه حقيقة ولهذا قيل : إن الديون تقضى بأمثالها على معنى أن المقبوض مضمون على القابض لأنه قبضه لنفسه على وجه التملك ولرب الدين على المدين مثله فالتقى الدينان قصاصاً فصار غيره حقيقة وشرعاً؛ أما الحقيقة فظاهر، وأما الشرع فلا حاجة إلى اسقاط اعتباره لأن التصرف في الدين قبل القبض جائز والمفلس بالتشديد رجل حكم القاضي بإفلاسه والمليء الغني؛ ذكره مسكين والله أعلم . تم الجزء الرابع من البحر ويليه الجزء الخامس وأوله كتاب الحدود
هنا على ما إذا كانت مسكونة لغيره، أما إذا كانت حالية فيحنث إذا لم تنقطع نسبتها عنه وإضافتها إليه تأمل قوله : (لا يحنث إلا أن يدل الدليل على دار الغلة كذا في النسخ والصواب حذف «لا» من قوله «لا يحنث» كما يدل عليه الظاهر والسباق والسياق وقد ذكر المسألة قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس حيث قال عازياً إلى الظهيرية: ولو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فدخل داراً مشتركة بينه وبين فلان، إن كان فلان يسكنها يحنث وإلا فلا. وذكر قبلها عازياً إلى المحيط : لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان وله دار يسكنها ودار غلة فدخل دار الغلة لا يحنث إذا لم يدل الدليل على دار الغلة وغيرها لأن داره مطلقاً دار يسكنها قوله : (كذا في الواقعات) أقول : يخالفه ما مر قبيل قوله ودوام الركوب واللبس الخ» معزياً إلى الخانية : لو حلف لا يدخل دار ابنته وابنته تسكن في دار زوجها أو حلف لا يدخل دار أمه وأمه تسكن في بيت زوجها فدخل الحالف حنث ا هـ . وكذا ذكر في النهر عند قول المتن وفي طاق الباب لا ما نصه : ولا فرق في الساكن بين ركوبه تبعاً أو حتى لو حلف إلى آخر ما ذكره في الخانية لكن ذكر في الخانية قبل هذه المسألة بنحو ورقتين عين الفرع المنقول هنا عن الواقعات. وقال في جوابه: إن لم ينو تلك الدار لا يحنث لأن السكني تضاف إلى الزوج لا إلى المرأة، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن الدار في المسألة المارة لما لم تكن ملكاً للمرأة أريدت السكنى بطريق التبعية، ولما كانت الدار في مسألتنا ملكاً لها انعقدت اليمين على السكني بالإصالة، ولما كان زوجها ساكناً معها صارت تبعاً له لأنها تضاف حينئذ إلى الزوج فلم يوجد شرط الحنث لكن رأيت في التتارخانية ما يفيد اختلاف الرواية حيث ذكر مسألة الواقعات ثم ذكر الثانية عن المنتقى ثم قال : وهذه الرواية تخالف ما ذكر . ثم ذكر عن الفضلي تفصيلاً وهو أنه إن لم يكن للمحلوف عليه دار أخرى ننسب إليه يحنث وإلا فلا. قال : ولم يذكر هذا في المنتقى ا.هـ. وفي البزازية بعد ذكره التفصيل المذكور قال : وفي المنتقى اختار الحنث مطلقاً اعتباراً بالمساكنة إلا إذا نوى داراً مملوكة لكل منهما ا هـ. والله سبحانه أعلم.
So he entered a house belonging to so-and-so and another, and so-and-so resides in it: he does not break his oath unless evidence indicates a rental property or other. And the text is absolute regarding ownership, so it includes a jointly owned house. Thus, if he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and enters a house jointly owned between so-and-so and another, and so-and-so resides in it, he breaks his oath because the entire house is attributed to him partly by ownership and wholly by residency. It is necessary that so-and-so's residency be not by way of dependency. Thus, if he swears not to enter so-and-so's house (a woman) and enters her house while her husband resides in it, he does not break his oath because the house is attributed to the resident, and the resident is the husband. This is as in al-Waqi'at, and we have presented it in the discussion of entry. His statement: (If he swears that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed by an insolvent or a wealthy person, he does not break his oath because a debt is not wealth; rather it is a description in the dhimmah that cannot actually be received. Thus it is said: Debts are repaid by their equivalents, meaning that what is received is a guarantee upon the receiver because he received it for himself by way of ownership, and the creditor has its equivalent against the debtor, so the two debts set off each other, becoming other than actual wealth both in reality and in law; as for reality, it is apparent; and as for law, there is no need to disregard it because transactions involving a debt before receipt are permissible. Al-Muflis (insolvent) stressed is a man whom the judge has declared insolvent, and al-mali' is the wealthy; mentioned by Miskin. And Allah knows best. The fourth volume of al-Bahr is completed, followed by the fifth volume, beginning with the Book of Hudud. Here regarding when it is inhabited by someone else, but if it is empty he breaks his oath if its attribution to him is not cut off and his addition to it. Consider his statement: (He does not break his oath unless evidence indicates a rental property) recorded in the copies, and the correct is to drop 'la' from his statement 'la yahanthu' as indicated by appearance, context, and sequence. He had mentioned the issue before his statement 'and continuity of riding and wearing' where he said, attributing to al-Zahiriya: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and enters a house jointly owned between him and so-and-so, if so-and-so resides in it he breaks his oath, otherwise no. And before it he mentioned, attributing to al-Muhit: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's house and he has a house he resides in and a rental house, then enters the rental house, he does not break his oath if evidence does not indicate the rental house or other, because his house absolutely is the one he resides in. His statement: (as in al-Waqi'at) I say: it is contradicted by what passed before his statement 'and continuity of riding and wearing' attributing to al-Khaniya: If he swears not to enter his daughter's house and his daughter resides in her husband's house, or swears not to enter his mother's house and his mother resides in her husband's house, then the swearer enters, he breaks his oath. And similar is mentioned in al-Nahr at the statement of the text 'and in the arch of the door, not what is stated: no difference in the resident between being a dependent or even if he swears...' until the rest of what al-Khaniya mentions. But al-Khaniya mentioned about two sheets before this issue the exact branch narrated here from al-Waqi'at. And he said in its answer: If he did not intend that house, he does not break his oath because residency is attributed to the husband, not to the woman. And it can be answered that the house in the previous issue, since it was not owned by the woman, residency was intended by way of dependency; and since the house in our issue is owned by her, the oath was made based on residency as original; and since her husband resides with her, she became dependent on him because then it is attributed to the husband, so the condition for breaking the oath is not met. But I saw in al-Tatarkhaniya something indicating a difference in narration: he mentioned the issue from al-Waqi'at, then mentioned the second from al-Muntaqa, then said: This narration contradicts what was mentioned. Then he mentioned from al-Fadli a detail: if the one sworn about has no other house to attribute to him, he breaks his oath; otherwise no. He said: This is not mentioned in al-Muntaqa. In al-Bazzaziya after mentioning the aforementioned detail, he said: In al-Muntaqa, he chose breaking the oath absolutely considering co-residence, unless he intended a house owned by each of them. And Allah Almighty knows best.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing two distinct rulings under the chapter of oaths concerning beating and killing. The first ruling deals with oaths not to enter a specific person's house. The author explains that if one swears not to enter 'so-and-so's house' and enters a house that is jointly owned by that person and another, the oath is broken only if so-and-so actually resides in that house. The reason is that the house is attributed to the resident, not just the owner. If the person does not live there (e.g., it is a rental property or empty), the oath is not broken. An exception is made for a woman: if one swears not to enter a woman's house and enters her house while her husband lives there, the oath is not broken because the house is attributed to the resident (the husband). The author then cites various sources (al-Waqi'at, al-Zahiriya, al-Muhit, al-Khaniya, al-Nahr, al-Tatarkhaniya, etc.) and discusses conflicting narrations, showing that there is ikhtilaf among later authorities. A detail from al-Fadli resolves the conflict by considering whether the person has another house to which they are attributed. The second ruling concerns oaths about having no wealth. If one swears that he has no wealth while he has a debt owed to him (whether from an insolvent or wealthy debtor), the oath is not broken because a debt is not considered wealth in the true sense; it is merely a description in the dhimma (liability) that cannot be physically received. The author explains that debts are repaid by equivalents, and upon receipt the creditor has a guarantee against the debtor, so the two debts offset. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that debts are not property for the purposes of oaths and zakat until actually received. The passage ends with a note about the completion of the fourth volume and the beginning of the fifth.

Key Terms
مليء (mali'): A wealthy person, capable of paying debts. Opposite of مفلس (insolvent).
مال (mal): Wealth, property that can be possessed and utilized. In Hanafi oaths, debts are not considered mal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I have no wealth.' He is owed $500 by a friend who is wealthy. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the debt is a financial asset.
B) No, because a debt is not considered wealth for oaths.
C) Yes, because the debtor is wealthy and can pay.
D) No, because the oath is invalid if he has any assets.

Key Takeaway
A debt is not considered wealth for the purpose of oaths; swearing to have no wealth is not violated by being owed a debt.
